The bane of political argument of late years has 
been the facility with which speculative predic- 
tions hare been accepted as unanswerable ccm- 
elusions of philosophy. A year or two ago, any 
one who presumed to 'oubt that .radical Parlia- 
mentary Heform was imminent would have been

only difficulty which hampered the resistance to 
Mr. Bright ! agitation was the concession which 
almost every one bad made, that, however un- 
palatable the fact might be, the time for a 
■weeping electoral revolution had already 
arrived. What had been 10 boldly asserted 
on the one side, and ao timidly admitted on 
the other, proved to be a complete fallacy, 
though the prediction came near to realise itself 
in consequence of in acceptance by the very 
men who regarded it with the utmost aversion. 
It is worth considering whether, in admitting 
the certainty of ultimate separation between 
England and her colonies, we are not falling 
into an error of the same kind, and playing 
into the hands of those who are anxioua to 
hasten the dlvorco which they repaid u in- 
evitable- In one sense, of course, it maybe 
laid that the connection between a mother- 
country and her colonies ranat come to an end, 
but «Bly i* the a&rne. sense in which, judging 
from eiptrrtence, the dissolution of the 
mightiiat and most compact empires 
may be anticipated u the natural 
consequence nf the continuous flow of events. 
No institutions ever had the stamp of per- 
manence more strongly marked upon them than 
those of our country, end yet few would vendue 
to say that Macaulny'a New Zsaiander may not 
witness their decay. Permanence cannot bs 
absolutely predicted of anything human, and 
least of all of political Institutions. In this 
sense, therefore, it may bo true in terms that the 
time for the mancipation of the colonica must 
surely come. But if it means no Bore than 
this, the usertion is the emptiest of platitudes ; 
ud if it does mean more, we believe that it is 
both uutrue and extremely misohievaai, inu- 
much as it places an almost impregnable 
position at the command of specula- 
tive politicians. What the Tim,, pro- 
bubly meant Is ssy by its unguarded admission 
was, that she connexion between England and 
her colonise was essentially provisional and 
temporary— that it could never ripen into any 
permanent union— and th»t, one by one, the 
colonies must be thrown off as they arrive m 
maturity, just u the children of it family eman- 
cipate themselves when they arrive at ream of 
diet, \tioo. 

If thin he accepted u true philosophy, the 
only answer that can possibly be given to Mr 
Ooldwin Smith is, that the event which he looks 
lor ™ the immediate future may perhaps be 
postponed for another fifty or a hundred 
yean. But, almost univsrsaJ u the 
notion of an ultimate severance 
between England and her colonies hu become, 
we believe that it depends much more on the

There are many waya besides separation ia 
which the institutions of our colonial empire 
might accommodate themselves to the rapid 
increase of wealth, population, and power 
which promises to attend upon our various 
colonies. Whether any of them will ever attain 
a relative importance sufficient to deprive 
England of her claim to a metropolitan posi- 
tion may well be doubted; but even In so 
extreme and remote a cue aa this, it might be 
quite u easy to readjust the old bond 
on terms of greater equality al to snap 
it altogether. Even the possibility of the 
friendly separation which is assumed to be the 
euthanasia of our colonial dominion i, by no 
means clear ; for the desire to separate, which 
must precede the divorce, could acarcely grow 
prednminant without something analogous to 
the irritation which an actual conflict would 
provoke. Certainly it is a bold assumption to 
take for granted the absolute certainty of a 
transaction the like of which hu never been 
atttmpted since the world began. It would 
perhaps be t«ss extravagant to imagine a con- 
tinual approach on the part of England 
and her colonies to the realisation of 
some idea of a Federal Empire, 
which the democratic machinery of the 
1 tailed Ptales has so signs!] y failed to construrt. 
Hut it is a 1 idle to trice one course as another for

dissolution are sown in our colonial empire is 
wholly improved, and that, while tbe tendency 
of colonial feeling continues to be more and more 
in favour of union, it is neither philosophical 
nor politic to assume the necessity of s 
future ssvsrance. The form of tbe con-

stance* of the colonist end the higher tone of

itself is not likely to cease so long u a common

to hind England and her colonies together. The 
arguments by which it is attempted to pove

practicability of any other,
